Maybe we can try to use a combination where the non-sunny lines are not all covering many points, but we have enough sunny lines to cover the rest. However, we need to cover all points, and the sunny lines have limited capacity. Let's analyze the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover in the triangle for given n. This is a key combinatorial constraint.

Let T_n = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }. For a line L with slope m (not 0, ∞, -1), the set of integer points on L is either empty or infinite if m is rational. But we only care about those in T_n. Let L contain integer points. Write L: y = (p/q)x + c, with p,q integers, gcd(p,q)=1, q>0, and m = p/q ≠ 0, ∞, -1. Then integer points on L are of the form (x0 + q t, y0 + p t) for some integer x0,y0. The condition a+b ≤ n+1 gives (x0+y0) + (p+q)t ≤ n+1. Since x0,y0 ≥ 1, x0+y0 ≥ 2. So for t ≥ 0, we need t ≤ (n+1 - (x0+y0))/(p+q). So the number of integer points on L in T_n is at most floor((n+1 - 2)/(p+q)) + 1? Actually t can be negative as well? If we allow t negative, then the points might be on the other side. But we can choose the parametrization so that the points in T_n correspond to t in some interval. The maximum number of integer points on L in T_n is achieved when the line passes through many points with small sum. The maximum possible number is when p+q is minimal and x0+y0 is minimal. Since p+q ≥ 2? For slopes not 0,∞,-1, p and q are integers, q>0, p≠0. The smallest p+q is 2, achieved when p=q=1 (slope 1) or p=-1,q=1? Actually p=-1,q=1 gives slope -1, which is excluded (since parallel to x+y=0). So p=q=1 gives slope 1, p+q=2. Also p=1,q=1 gives slope 1, p= -1,q=1 gives slope -1, excluded. p=2,q=1 gives p+q=3, etc. So the minimal p+q is 2, giving slope 1. So the maximum number of integer points on a sunny line is when slope = 1, and the line goes through points (1,1), (2,2), ..., up to some maximum. For a line y = x + c, the integer points are (a, a+c). The sum a+b = 2a + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ floor((n+1-c)/2). Also a ≥ 1. So the number of points is floor((n+1-c)/2) - 1 + 1 = floor((n+1-c)/2). For c=0, we get floor((n+1)/2). For c=1, floor((n)/2). For c negative, we get more points? For c = -1, floor((n+2)/2) = floor((n+2)/2). But note that a must be such that b = a-1 ≥ 1 => a ≥ 2. So the points are (2,1), (3,2), ..., up to a_max. The number of points is floor((n+2)/2) - 1? Actually if c=-1, then points (a, a-1) with a≥2, and a+b = 2a-1 ≤ n+1 => a ≤ (n+2)/2. So a from 2 to floor((n+2)/2). Number = floor((n+2)/2) - 1. For n=5, floor((7)/2)=3, so a=2,3 gives 2 points. For c=0, a=1..floor(6/2)=3 gives 3 points. So the maximum number of points on a line of slope 1 is floor((n+1)/2) when c=0. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3? Actually (n+1)/2 = 3.5, floor=3. For n=7, floor(8/2)=4. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). But wait, we also have lines with slope not 1 that might have more points? For slope 2, p+q=3, so maximum points is about n/3. So indeed slope 1 gives the most points. So the maximum points a sunny line can cover in T_n is M = floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, M=2; n=4, M=2; n=5, M=3; n=6, M=3; n=7, M=4; etc.

Now, we have n lines total. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * M points, but they might overlap. The non-sunny lines can cover many points as well. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. We need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Each such line, if it passes through integer points, can cover at most n points (for a horizontal line y=b, it covers n+1-b points, which is at most n when b=1). Similarly, vertical line x=a covers at most n points (when a=1). Slope -1 line with sum c covers c-1 points, which is at most n when c=n+1 (since a+b=n+1 gives n points). So each non-sunny line can cover up to n points. But they can also cover fewer points if placed at other positions.

We already tested some: y=1 (4) and x=2 (3) gives 7, not 6. Also, x=1 (4) with y=2 (2) gives 6; x=1 with x=3 (2) gives 6; x=1 with c=3 (2) gives 6; x=1 with y=3? That's 4+1=5; x=1 with c=2 gives 5. So these are possible.

Now, also pairs of lines each covering 3: x=2 (3) with c=4 (3) gives 6; x=2 with c=5 (3) gives 6; c=4 with c=5 gives 6; also x=2 with x=2? Not distinct. So these are possible.

Also, could have 2+4, 3+3, etc.

We need to test each such pair and see if the uncovered points can be covered by two sunny lines.

Let's compute uncovered points for each candidate.

First, list all points: P1(1,1), P2(1,2), P3(1,3), P4(1,4), P5(2,1), P6(2,2), P7(2,3), P8(3,1), P9(3,2), P10(4,1).

Candidate A: L1 = y=1 (covers P1,P5,P8,P10). L2 = y=2 (covers P2,P6). Union: P1,P2,P5,P6,P8,P10. Uncovered: P3(1,3), P4(1,4), P7(2,3), P9(3,2). So uncovered set U = {P3,P4,P7,P9}. Thus for n=5, k=2 might be impossible. But we haven't exhausted all possible triples of non-sunny lines. There are many. However, we can try to see if there is a pattern. For n=3, k=2 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible; for n=5, maybe k=2 is also impossible? But we need to determine all k for given n≥3. The problem likely expects a characterization: k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? But we also saw that for n=3, k=4 is impossible because not enough capacity. So the set of possible k depends on n. For n=3, max sunny lines =3 (since we have 3 lines, and we can have all sunny). For n=4, max sunny lines =3? Actually we had k=3 possible, but k=4 impossible. So the maximum k is floor(n(n+1)/2 / M) maybe? But we need to cover all points, so the number of sunny lines must be such that the total coverage capacity (sum of maximum points per line) is at least N. But we also need that the non-sunny lines can cover many points. In general, each sunny line can cover at most M = floor((n+1)/2) points. Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So if we have k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines, the total sum of maximum points is kM + (n-k)n. This must be at least N = n(n+1)/2. That gives a necessary condition: kM + (n-k)n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => kM + n^2 - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2 => k(M - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2 - n^2 = n(n+1-2n)/2 = n(1-n)/2 = -n(n-1)/2 => k(n - M) ≤ n(n-1)/2. Since n - M = n - floor((n+1)/2). For n odd, n=2m+1, then M = floor((2m+2)/2)=m+1, so n-M = 2m+1 - (m+1)=m. For n even, n=2m, then M = floor((2m+1)/2)=m, so n-M = 2m - m = m. So n-M = floor(n/2). So condition: k * floor(n/2) ≤ n(n-1)/2. This is always true for k up to n? For n=3, floor(3/2)=1, n(n-1)/2=3, so k≤3. For n=4, floor(4/2)=2, n(n-1)/2=6, so k≤3. So k=4 fails the necessary condition. So the maximum k is floor(n(n-1)/(2 floor(n/2))) = floor((n(n-1))/(2 floor(n/2))). For n=3, that's floor(6/2)=3; for n=4, floor(12/4)=3; for n=5, floor(20/2)=10? Actually floor(5/2)=2, n(n-1)/2=10, so k≤5; but we have only 5 lines, so k=5 is possible if the capacity condition holds. For n=5, M=3, n-M=2, condition k*2 ≤10 => k≤5, so k=5 is allowed by this necessary condition. But we need to check if k=5 is actually achievable. For n=5, can we cover 15 points with 5 sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so 5 sunny lines can cover at most 15 points if they are disjoint. So we need to partition the 15 points into 5 sets of 3 points each, each set collinear on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1. Is that possible? The triangle points: we need to find 5 lines, each with slope not 0,∞,-1, each containing exactly 3 points from T_5, and these lines are distinct, and their union covers all points. This is a strong combinatorial design. Possibly it is possible. For example, we can try to use lines of slope 1: y=x+c. For c=0, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum6 allowed? The other lines would have at most 2 points. So 5 lines cannot cover 15 points if each has at most 2 points except one with 3. So k=5 is impossible. So the necessary condition is not sufficient.

Thus we need a more precise characterization. The problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). The answer likely is: k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? The sunny lines can cover up to 3 points each, so 2 sunny lines can cover at most 6 points. So total coverage capacity is 21, but we need to cover 15 points. The union of the 3 non-sunny lines could be as large as 15 if they are disjoint. But we also have 2 sunny lines that can cover some points. The question is whether we can choose 3 non-sunny lines such that the complement can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Since the complement size must be at most 6, but also must be coverable by 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, the complement size could be up to 6. But we can also have the sunny lines covering points that are already covered by non-sunny lines, so the complement is the set of points not covered by non-sunny lines. So we need to find 3 non-sunny lines whose union misses at most 6 points, and those missing points can be covered by 2 sunny lines.

(1,5)-(2,4) slope -1, no; (1,5)-(4,2) slope -1; (2,4)-(4,2) slope -1; so no. Could we use a sunny line that covers (1,5) and (2,4) and (3,3)? No. So fails.

Thus for n=5, k=2 seems impossible. For n=6, we can test similarly. The pattern suggests that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. Also, we need to consider k values that are too large, like k=n, but we have capacity constraints. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following." So the answer likely is: k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? But we also saw that for n=4, k=4 is impossible because capacity. For n=5, k=5 is impossible. So the maximum possible k is not n for n≥4. Actually for n=3, k=3 is possible. For n=4, k=3 is possible, but k=4 is impossible. For n=5, what is the maximum k? We need to check if k=3 is possible for n=5. For n=5, we have 5 lines. Could we have 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines covering 15 points? Possibly yes. For example, use y=1 (5 points) and x=1 (5 points) as non-sunny, and then 3 sunny lines to cover the remaining 5 points? But we saw that after y=1 and x=1, uncovered points are 6 points. So we need 3 sunny lines to cover 6 points. Each sunny line can cover up to 3 points, so 3 sunny lines can cover at most 9 points, but we need to cover 6 points. However, we need to see if we can cover those 6 points with 3 sunny lines. The uncovered set U = { (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2) }. Can we find 3 sunny lines that together contain all these points? Each sunny line can contain at most 3 points from U. Since there are 6 points, we need to partition them into 3 sets of 2 points each, each set collinear on a sunny line. Is that possible? Let's try to find pairs that are on a sunny line. We already have some sunny pairs: (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal? No, that's horizontal y=2, not sunny. (2,2)-(2,3) vertical, not sunny. (2,2)-(4,2) horizontal. (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1, not sunny. (2,3)-(4,2) slope -1/2, sunny. (2,4)-(3,2) slope -2, sunny. (2,4)-(4,2) slope -1, not sunny. (3,2)-(3,3) vertical, not sunny. (3,2)-(4,2) horizontal. (3,3)-(4,2) slope -1/2, sunny. So we need to cover all 6 points with 3 sunny lines, each line covering at least 2 points from U. Since there are 6 points, we need to pair them up into 3 pairs, each pair must be a sunny pair. But we only have three sunny pairs: (2,3)-(4,2), (2,4)-(3,2), (3,3)-(4,2). But note that (4,2) appears in two pairs: with (2,3) and with (3,3). So we cannot assign both pairs to separate lines because (4,2) can only be on one line. So we need to cover all points. Could we use a line that covers three points? Is there a line that contains three points from U? We already checked none. So the only way to cover all points with 3 sunny lines is to have each line cover exactly 2 points, and the pairs must be disjoint. But the three sunny pairs are not disjoint; they share (4,2). So we cannot have three disjoint sunny pairs covering all 6 points. Could we use a sunny line that covers one point from U and also some other points not in U? That doesn't help cover the points in U. So we need to cover each point in U by at least one sunny line. If we use a line through (4,2) and (2,3), that covers (4,2) and (2,3). Then we still need to cover (2,4),(3,2),(3,3). Could we use a line through (2,4) and (3,2) covering (2,4) and (3,2). Then left (3,3) uncovered. Could we use a line through (3,3) and (4,2)? That would be the same as the first line? Actually (3,3)-(4,2) is a different line, but it would also cover (4,2) again, but (4,2) is already covered. That line would cover (3,3) and (4,2). Then we have two lines covering (4,2) and (3,3), and the third line covering (2,4) and (3,2). But we haven't tried other combinations of non-sunny lines. Perhaps we can use a different set of non-sunny lines that leaves a set of points that can be covered by 3 sunny lines. We have |S| ≤ 10 (since two lines max 5 each, but they can overlap, so |S| can be as low as 1 if they are the same line? But they must be distinct, so they can be different lines. The maximum |S| is 10 if they are disjoint. So |S| can be 1 to 10. Then |T| ≤ 9, but T may intersect S. So we need |S| + |T| - |S∩T| = 15. Since |T| ≤ 9, we have |S| ≥ 6. So the non-sunny lines must cover at least 6 points. That's easy.

But we also need that the sunny lines are distinct and sunny. The problem is to find if there exists any configuration. We can try to construct one.

We need to solve an olympiad problem about lines covering points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1, and exactly k of the n lines are "sunny". A line is sunny if it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, undefined (vertical), or -1. So sunny lines are those with slope not 0, not infinite, not -1. So they are lines with slope ≠ 0, ∞, -1. The lines are distinct.

We need to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying: for all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. And exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

We need to find possible k for given n≥3.

Interpretation: We have a set of n lines. They must cover all lattice points (a,b) with a,b≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1. That's a triangular region of integer points. The lines are arbitrary (not necessarily through lattice points). The condition is that each such point lies on at least one of the lines.

We want exactly k of the lines to be sunny (i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0). The rest are non-sunny, i.e., they are parallel to one of those three directions: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

We need to determine for which k there exists a configuration of n lines covering the triangular set.

We need to consider constraints. The number of lines n is given, and we can choose them arbitrarily. The covering condition is quite strong. The triangular region has many points: number of points = sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = (n(n+1)/2) - 1? Actually positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1: a from 1 to n, b from 1 to n+1-a. So total points = sum_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = n(n+1)/2 - n? Let's compute: sum_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = n(n+1) - sum_{a=1}^{n} a = n(n+1) - n(n+1)/2 = n(n+1)/2. That's number of points? For a=1, b max = n, so b=1..n gives n points; a=2 gives n-1 points; ... a=n gives 1 point. So total = n + (n-1)+...+1 = n(n+1)/2. Yes, that's number of lattice points in the triangle with vertices (1,1), (n,1), (1,n). Actually a+b ≤ n+1, so the triangle includes points on the line a+b = n+1 as well. So total points = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines. Each line can cover many points. But we need to cover all points. Since n is relatively small compared to the number of points, the lines must be placed strategically to cover many points. Possibly we can use lines that are "grid lines" (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) to cover many points. But they are non-sunny. Sunny lines are those that are not parallel to these three directions, so they have slopes that are not 0, ∞, -1. They can still cover points, but maybe they are less efficient in covering many points? Actually a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 can also cover many points if it passes through many lattice points, but it's limited. However, we are free to choose lines arbitrarily, not necessarily through lattice points. The condition is that each point lies on at least one line. So we can use lines that are not through lattice points to cover points? But a point lies on a line if the line passes through that point. So to cover a point, the line must pass through it. So each line is a set of points that lie on it. For a given line, the set of integer points it covers is either empty, or if it passes through at least one integer point, it may pass through infinitely many if its slope is rational, but if slope is irrational, it passes through at most one integer point? Actually a line with irrational slope can pass through at most one integer point because if it passes through two distinct integer points, the slope would be rational. So to cover many integer points, the line should have rational slope. But we are not required to have lines through integer points; we can choose lines that pass through many of the points. However, the covering condition is only for the specific set of points. So we can design lines that each pass through many of these points.

We need to determine possible k. Possibly k can be any integer from 0 to n? But there might be restrictions. Let's explore.

We have n lines. The non-sunny lines are those parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So they are horizontal lines (y = constant), vertical lines (x = constant), or lines with slope -1 (y = -x + c). These lines, if they pass through integer points, can cover many points. For example, a horizontal line y = b covers all points with that b and a from 1 to n+1-b. So it covers (n+1-b) points. Similarly, a vertical line x = a covers points with that a and b from 1 to n+1-a, covering (n+1-a) points. A line of slope -1: y = -x + c. For integer points (a,b) on it, we need b = -a + c => a+b = c. So it covers points with a+b = c. For c between 2 and n+1, the number of points with a+b = c is c-1 (since a from 1 to c-1, b = c-a). So it covers c-1 points.

So these lines can cover many points. Sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1. They can also cover points if they pass through multiple integer points, but then the slope is rational. For a line with rational slope p/q in lowest terms, it can cover infinitely many integer points if it passes through one integer point, but the set of integer points on it is an arithmetic progression. However, the points we need to cover are only those in the triangle. So a sunny line could cover several points along a line that goes through the triangle. But perhaps the maximum number of points a line can cover in this triangle is limited. For a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1, the maximum number of lattice points it can intersect in the triangle might be at most n? Actually consider a line that goes through many points: e.g., line y = x (slope 1) passes through points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (n,n) but note that a+b ≤ n+1, so for n≥3, (n,n) gives a+b=2n > n+1, so only points with a=b and a+b ≤ n+1 => 2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So it covers at most floor((n+1)/2) points. That's about n/2. A line with slope 2: y=2x, then points (1,2), (2,4), ... but a+b ≤ n+1 => 1+2=3, 2+4=6, etc. So it covers at most floor((n+1)/3) maybe. So the maximum number of points a line can cover in this triangle is when the line is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1? Actually horizontal line y=b covers n+1-b points, which can be as large as n (when b=1 gives n points). Vertical line x=a covers n+1-a points, max n when a=1. Maybe we can try to use a combination where the non-sunny lines are not all covering many points, but we have enough sunny lines to cover the rest. However, we need to cover all points, and the sunny lines have limited capacity. Let's analyze the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover in the triangle for given n. This is a key combinatorial constraint.

Let T_n = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }. For a line L with slope m (not 0, ∞, -1), the set of integer points on L is either empty or infinite if m is rational. But we only care about those in T_n. Let L contain integer points. Write L: y = (p/q)x + c, with p,q integers, gcd(p,q)=1, q>0, and m = p/q ≠ 0, ∞, -1. Then integer points on L are of the form (x0 + q t, y0 + p t) for some integer x0,y0. The condition a+b ≤ n+1 gives (x0+y0) + (p+q)t ≤ n+1. Since x0,y0 ≥ 1, x0+y0 ≥ 2. So for t ≥ 0, we need t ≤ (n+1 - (x0+y0))/(p+q). So the number of integer points on L in T_n is at most floor((n+1 - 2)/(p+q)) + 1? Actually t can be negative as well? If we allow t negative, then the points might be on the other side. But we can choose the parametrization so that the points in T_n correspond to t in some interval. The maximum number of integer points on L in T_n is achieved when the line passes through many points with small sum. The maximum possible number is when p+q is minimal and x0+y0 is minimal. Since p+q ≥ 2? For slopes not 0,∞,-1, p and q are integers, q>0, p≠0. The smallest p+q is 2, achieved when p=q=1 (slope 1) or p=-1,q=1? Actually p=-1,q=1 gives slope -1, which is excluded (since parallel to x+y=0). So p=q=1 gives slope 1, p+q=2. Also p=1,q=1 gives slope 1, p= -1,q=1 gives slope -1, excluded. p=2,q=1 gives p+q=3, etc. So the minimal p+q is 2, giving slope 1. So the maximum number of integer points on a sunny line is when slope = 1, and the line goes through points (1,1), (2,2), ..., up to some maximum. For a line y = x + c, the integer points are (a, a+c). The sum a+b = 2a + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ floor((n+1-c)/2). Also a ≥ 1. So the number of points is floor((n+1-c)/2) - 1 + 1 = floor((n+1-c)/2). For c=0, we get floor((n+1)/2). For c=1, floor((n)/2). For c negative, we get more points? For c = -1, floor((n+2)/2) = floor((n+2)/2). But note that a must be such that b = a-1 ≥ 1 => a ≥ 2. So the maximum number of points on a line of slope 1 is floor((n+1)/2) when c=0. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3? Actually (n+1)/2 = 3.5, floor=3. For n=7, floor(8/2)=4. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). But wait, we also have lines with slope not 1 that might have more points? For slope 2, p+q=3, so maximum points is about n/3. So indeed slope 1 gives the most points. So the maximum points a sunny line can cover in T_n is M = floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, M=2; n=4, M=2; n=5, M=3; n=6, M=3; n=7, M=4; etc.

Now, we have n lines total. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * M points, but they might overlap. The non-sunny lines can cover many points as well. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. We need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Each such line, if it passes through integer points, can cover at most n points (for a horizontal line y=b, it covers n+1-b points, which is at most n when b=1). Similarly, vertical line x=a covers at most n points (when a=1). Slope -1 line with sum c covers c-1 points, which is at most n when c=n+1 (since a+b=n+1 gives n points). So each non-sunny line can cover up to n points. But they can also cover fewer points if placed at other positions.

We need to determine for which k there exists a set of n lines (distinct) covering T_n, with exactly k sunny lines. This is a covering problem. We can think of constructing such a configuration. The examples for n=3 show that k=0,1,3 are possible, but k=2 is not. For n=4, we need to test if k=2 is possible. Let's try to construct a configuration for n=4 with k=2. Thus for n=5, k=2 might be impossible. But we haven't exhausted all possible triples of non-sunny lines. There are many. However, we can try to see if there is a pattern. For n=3, k=2 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible; for n=5, maybe k=2 is also impossible? But we need to determine all k for given n≥3. The problem likely expects a characterization: k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? But we also saw that for n=3, k=4 is impossible because not enough capacity. So the set of possible k depends on n. For n=3, max sunny lines =3 (since we have 3 lines, and we can have all sunny). For n=4, max sunny lines =3? Actually we had k=3 possible, but k=4 impossible. So the maximum k is floor(n(n+1)/2 / M) maybe? But we need to cover all points, so the number of sunny lines must be such that the total coverage capacity (sum of maximum points per line) is at least N. But we also need that the non-sunny lines can cover many points. In general, each sunny line can cover at most M = floor((n+1)/2) points. Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So if we have k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines, the total sum of maximum points is kM + (n-k)n. This must be at least N = n(n+1)/2. That gives a necessary condition: kM + (n-k)n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => kM + n^2 - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2 => k(M - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2 - n^2 = n(n+1-2n)/2 = n(1-n)/2 = -n(n-1)/2 => k(n - M) ≤ n(n-1)/2. Since n - M = n - floor((n+1)/2). For n odd, n=2m+1, then M = floor((2m+2)/2)=m+1, so n-M = 2m+1 - (m+1)=m. For n even, n=2m, then M = floor((2m+1)/2)=m, so n-M = 2m - m = m. So n-M = floor(n/2). So condition: k * floor(n/2) ≤ n(n-1)/2. This is always true for k up to n? For n=3, floor(3/2)=1, n(n-1)/2=3, so k≤3. For n=4, floor(4/2)=2, n(n-1)/2=6, so k≤3. So k=4 fails the necessary condition. So the maximum k is floor(n(n-1)/(2 floor(n/2))) = floor((n(n-1))/(2 floor(n/2))). For n=3, that's floor(6/2)=3; for n=4, floor(12/4)=3; for n=5, floor(20/2)=10? Actually floor(5/2)=2, n(n-1)/2=10, so k≤5; but we have only 5 lines, so k=5 is possible if the capacity condition holds. For n=5, M=3, n-M=2, condition k*2 ≤10 => k≤5, so k=5 is allowed by this necessary condition. But we need to check if k=5 is actually achievable. For n=5, can we cover 15 points with 5 sunny lines? Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so 5 sunny lines can cover at most 15 points if they are disjoint. So we need to partition the 15 points into 5 sets of 3 points each, each set collinear on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1. Is that possible? The triangle points: we need to find 5 lines, each with slope not 0,∞,-1, each containing exactly 3 points from T_5, and these lines are distinct, and their union covers all points. This is a strong combinatorial design. Possibly it is possible. For example, we can try to use lines of slope 1: y=x+c. For c=0, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum6 allowed? So 3 sunny lines can cover at most 9 points, but we need to cover 7. However, we need to see if there exist 3 sunny lines that together contain all these 7 points. Is there a line that contains 3 of them? Possibly. Let's examine U = { (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,2) }. Check collinear triples:
- (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2) are horizontal y=2, but (1,2) and (2,2) and (3,2) and (4,2) are all in U? (1,2) yes, (2,2) yes, (3,2) yes, (4,2) yes. So that's 4 points on a horizontal line. But horizontal lines are not sunny. So that line is not allowed as a sunny line. So we cannot use that.
- (1,2),(1,3),(1,4) vertical x=1, not sunny.
- (2,2),(2,3) vertical x=2, but only 2 points.
- (1,2),(2,3) slope (3-2)/(2-1)=1, allowed. (1,2),(2,3) are two points. Could there be a third point on that line? The line y=x+1. At x=3, y=4, not in U; at x=0, y=1 not. So no.
- (1,2),(3,2) horizontal, not sunny.
- (1,3),(2,2) slope (2-3)/(2-1)=-1, not sunny.
- (1,3),(3,2) slope (2-3)/(3-1)=-1/2, allowed. (1,3),(3,2) are two points. Could there be a third? Line through (1,3) and (3,2): equation y-3 = -1/2(x-1) => y = -1/2 x + 3.5. At x=2, y=2.5 not in U; at x=4, y=0.5 no. So no.
- (1,4),(2,3) slope (3-4)/(2-1)=-1, not sunny.
- (1,4),(3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1)=-2/2=-1, not sunny.
- (2,2),(3,2) horizontal, not sunny.
- (2,2),(4,2) horizontal.
- (2,3),(3,2) slope -1, not sunny.
- (2,3),(4,2) slope (2-3)/(4-2)=-1/2, allowed. (2,3),(4,2) are two points. Could there be a third? Line through (2,3) and (4,2): slope -1/2, equation y-3 = -1/2(x-2) => y = -1/2 x + 4. At x=1, y=3.5 no; at x=3, y=2.5 no. So no.
- (1,2),(2,2) horizontal, no.
- (1,2),(1,3) vertical, no.
- (1,2),(3,2) horizontal, no.
- (1,3),(2,2) slope -1, no.
- (1,3),(4,2) slope (2-3)/(4-1)=-1/3, allowed. (1,3),(4,2) are two points. Could there be a third? Line: y-3 = -1/3(x-1) => y = -1/3 x + 10/3. At x=2, y= -2/3+10/3=8/3≈2.667 no; at x=3, y= -1+10/3=7/3≈2.333 no. So no.
- (1,4),(2,2) slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2, allowed. (1,4),(2,2) are two points. Could there be a third? Line: y-4 = -2(x-1) => y = -2x+6. At x=3, y=0, not; at x=4, y=-2, no. So no.
- (1,4),(3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1)=-2/2=-1, not sunny.
- (2,3),(3,2) slope -1, no.
- (2,3),(4,2) slope -1/2, allowed.
- (3,2),(4,2) horizontal.
- (1,2),(3,2) horizontal.
- (1,3),(3,2) slope -1/2.
- (1,2),(2,3) slope 1.
- (1,3),(2,2) slope -1.
- (1,4),(2,3) slope -1.

- (3,2),(4,2) horizontal.

- (1,3),(4,2) slope -1/3.

- (2,2),(4,2) horizontal.

Let's attempt a proof: Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines covering the triangular set T_n. We need to derive a contradiction. Perhaps we can use a counting argument on the number of points on each line, or use a combinatorial geometry argument.

Observation: Each non-sunny line is parallel to one of the three forbidden directions: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Such a line, if it passes through integer points, can cover at most n points (for horizontal or vertical) or n points (for slope -1). But we can also consider that the points on a non-sunny line are collinear with a specific direction.

The sunny lines have slopes not 0,∞,-1. They can cover at most M = floor((n+1)/2) points from T_n. Since we have only 2 sunny lines, the total number of points covered by sunny lines is at most 2M. But the union of all lines must cover all N = n(n+1)/2 points. So the non-sunny lines must cover at least N - 2M points. However, we also have that each non-sunny line can cover at most n points, and there are n-2 of them, so the maximum points they can cover is (n-2)n. So we need N - 2M ≤ (n-2)n. This gives a necessary condition: n(n+1)/
